<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-scale genome assembly of a diploid alfalfa, the progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-12-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.) is one of the most important and widely cultivated forage crops. It is commonly used as a vegetable and medicinal herb because of its excellent nutritional quality and significant economic value. Based on Illumina, Nanopore and Hi-C data, we assembled a chromosome-scale assembly of Medicago sativa spp. caerulea (voucher PI464715), the direct diploid progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa. The assembled genome comprises 793.2&#8201;Mb of genomic sequence and 47,202 annotated protein-coding genes. The contig N50 length is 3.86&#8201;Mb. This genome is almost twofold larger and contains more annotated protein-coding genes than that of its close relative, Medicago truncatula (420&#8201;Mb and 44,623 genes). The more expanded gene families compared with those in M. truncatula and the expansion of repetitive elements rather than whole-genome duplication (i.e., the two species share the ancestral Papilionoideae whole-genome duplication event) may have contributed to the large genome size of M. sativa spp. caerulea. Comparative and evolutionary analyses revealed that M. sativa spp. caerulea diverged from M. truncatula ~5.2 million years ago, and the chromosomal fissions and fusions detected between the two genomes occurred during the divergence of the two species. In addition, we identified 489 resistance (R) genes and 82 and 85 candidate genes involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways, respectively. The near-complete and accurate diploid alfalfa reference genome obtained herein serves as an important complement to the recently assembled autotetraploid alfalfa genome and will provide valuable genomic resources for investigating the genomic architecture of autotetraploid alfalfa as well as for improving breeding strategies in alfalfa."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.) is one of the most important and widely cultivated forage crops. It is commonly used as a vegetable and medicinal herb because of its excellent nutritional quality and significant economic value. Based on Illumina, Nanopore and Hi-C data, we assembled a chromosome-scale assembly of Medicago sativa spp. caerulea (voucher PI464715), the direct diploid progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa. The assembled genome comprises 793.2&#8201;Mb of genomic sequence and 47,202 annotated protein-coding genes. The contig N50 length is 3.86&#8201;Mb. This genome is almost twofold larger and contains more annotated protein-coding genes than that of its close relative, Medicago truncatula (420&#8201;Mb and 44,623 genes). The more expanded gene families compared with those in M. truncatula and the expansion of repetitive elements rather than whole-genome duplication (i.e., the two species share the ancestral Papilionoideae whole-genome duplication event) may have contributed to the large genome size of M. sativa spp. caerulea. Comparative and evolutionary analyses revealed that M. sativa spp. caerulea diverged from M. truncatula ~5.2 million years ago, and the chromosomal fissions and fusions detected between the two genomes occurred during the divergence of the two species. In addition, we identified 489 resistance (R) genes and 82 and 85 candidate genes involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways, respectively. The near-complete and accurate diploid alfalfa reference genome obtained herein serves as an important complement to the recently assembled autotetraploid alfalfa genome and will provide valuable genomic resources for investigating the genomic architecture of autotetraploid alfalfa as well as for improving breeding strategies in alfalfa."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i> L.) is one of the most important and widely cultivated forage crops. It is commonly used as a vegetable and medicinal herb because of its excellent nutritional quality and significant economic value. Based on Illumina, Nanopore and Hi-C data, we assembled a chromosome-scale assembly of <i>Medicago sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i> (voucher PI464715), the direct diploid progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa. The assembled genome comprises 793.2âMb of genomic sequence and 47,202 annotated protein-coding genes. The contig N50 length is 3.86âMb. This genome is almost twofold larger and contains more annotated protein-coding genes than that of its close relative, <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (420âMb and 44,623 genes). The more expanded gene families compared with those in <i>M. truncatula</i> and the expansion of repetitive elements rather than whole-genome duplication (i.e., the two species share the ancestral Papilionoideae whole-genome duplication event) may have contributed to the large genome size of <i>M. sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i>. Comparative and evolutionary analyses revealed that <i>M. sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i> diverged from <i>M. truncatula</i> ~5.2 million years ago, and the chromosomal fissions and fusions detected between the two genomes occurred during the divergence of the two species. In addition, we identified 489 resistance (<i>R</i>) genes and 82 and 85 candidate genes involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways, respectively. The near-complete and accurate diploid alfalfa reference genome obtained herein serves as an important complement to the recently assembled autotetraploid alfalfa genome and will provide valuable genomic resources for investigating the genomic architecture of autotetraploid alfalfa as well as for improving breeding strategies in alfalfa.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i> ssp<i>. sativa</i> L.) is a perennial legume forage that is widely cultivated for hay, pasture and silage production (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig1">1a</a>). As one of the most economically valuable crops in the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, Q. et al. MYB transcription factors in alfalfa (Medicago sativa): genome-wide identification and expression analysis under abiotic stresses. PeerJ 7, e7714 (2019)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d323e598">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, Z. et al. Global transcriptome sequencing using the Illumina platform and the development of EST-SSR markers in autotetraploid alfalfa. PLoS ONE 8, e83549 (2013)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d323e598_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Li, X. &amp; Brummer, E. C. Applied genetics and genomics in alfalfa breeding. Agronomy 2, 40â61 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d323e601">3</a></sup>, alfalfa has total estimated annual sales ranging from 7.8 to 10.8 billion dollars in the USA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="United States Department of Agriculture-National Agriculture Statistics Service. Crop Production Historical Track Records, April 2018. &#xA;                  https://downloads.usda.library.cornell.edu/usda-esmis/files/c534fn92g/6q182n624/v405sd06x/htrcp-04-12-2018.pdf&#xA;                  &#xA;                . (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d323e605">4</a></sup>. Alfalfa is known as âthe queen of forage cropsâ not only because of its high-protein content and nutritive value as an animal feed but also because of its atmospheric nitrogen (N) fixation capacity. It is used as a rotation crop to increase soil fertility and serves as an important habitat for wildlife<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Russelle, M. P. &amp; Birr, A. S. Large-Scale assessment of symbiotic dinitrogen fixation by crops. Agron. J. 96, 1754â1760 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d323e610">5</a></sup>. In addition, alfalfa is well known for its superior contents of vitamins (A, C, E, and K), protein and minerals, such as calcium, potassium, phosphorus, and iron<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bora, K. S. &amp; Sharma, A. Phytochemical and pharmacological potential of Medicago sativa: a review. Pharm. Biol. 49, 211â220 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d323e614">6</a></sup>. Its seed sprouts and tender tips contain all these nutrients but few calories and are often used as edible vegetables (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig1">1b</a>). Furthermore, alfalfa has long been used as a medicinal herb. Its seeds or dried leaves can be used as a nutritional supplement and are sold as a bulk powdered herb, capsules, and tablets in health food stores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brinker, F. Herb Contraindications and Drug Interactions. Eclectic Medical Publications (Eclectic Medical Publications, 2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d323e621">7</a></sup>. The extracts from alfalfa seeds and leaves have hypocholesterolemic, neuroprotective, antioxidant, hypolipidemic, and antimicrobial effects and are used in the treatment of diabetes, stroke, cancer and menopausal symptoms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bora, K. S. &amp; Sharma, A. Phytochemical and pharmacological potential of Medicago sativa: a review. Pharm. Biol. 49, 211â220 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d323e625">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Malinow, M. R., McLaughlin, P., Naito, H. K., Lewis, L. A. &amp; McNulty, W. P. Effect of alfalfa meal on shrinkage (regression) of atherosclerotic plaques during cholesterol feeding in monkeys. Atherosclerosis 30, 27â43 (1978)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d323e628">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Malinow, M. R., McLaughlin, P. &amp; Stafford, C. Alfalfa seeds: effects on cholesterol metabolism. Experientia 36, 562â564 (1980)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d323e628_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Seida, A., El-Hefnawy, H., Abou-Hussein, D., Mokhtar, F. A. &amp; Abdel-Naim, A. Evaluation of Medicago sativa L. sprouts as antihyperlipidemic and antihyperglycemic agent. Pak. J. Pharm. Sci. 28, 2061â2074 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d323e628_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sadeghi, L., Tanwir, F. &amp; Yousefi, B. V. Antioxidant effects of alfalfa can improve iron oxide nanoparticle damage: Invivo and invitro studies. Regul. Toxicol. Pharmacol. 81, 39â46 (2016)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d323e628_3">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hong, Y. H., Chao, W. W., Chen, M. L. &amp; Lin, B. F. Ethyl acetate extracts of alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.) sprouts inhibit lipopolysaccharide-induced inflammation in vitro and in vivo. J. Biomed. Sci. 16, 64 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d323e631">12</a></sup> (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig1">1c</a>). Alfalfa also exhibits a relatively high level of disease resistance potential compared to that of other food crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhang, C. &amp; Shi, S. Physiological and Proteomic Responses of Contrasting Alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.) Varieties to PEG-Induced Osmotic Stress. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 242 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d323e639">13</a></sup>. Therefore, it provides disease prevention between planting stages and increases the stock carrying capacity. In China, the cultivated area of alfalfa reached 3.6 million hectares in 2017; however, China still imports more than 1.3 million tons per year, accounting for ~85% of the total imported hay. An increasing industrial demand, low production and a lack of multiple improved varieties with strong resistance and quality may be some of the factors accounting for such a large supply gap in the alfalfa industry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pan, X. et al. Current Situation and Prospect of Alfalfa Industry. J. Green Sci. Technol. 4, 104â107 (2017). (in Chinese)" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d323e643">14</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Photographs of alfalfa uses and products."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Photographs of alfalfa uses and products.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="479" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Photograph of alfalfa used as a widely cultivated forage crop. <b>b</b> Photograph of alfalfa as food. <b>c</b> Photograph of alfalfa as a health product. Picture from Dr. Willmar Schwabe, India Pvt., Ltd.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>On the basis of advanced sequencing technologies, breeders can use DNA markers combined with genome sequences to facilitate gene discovery, trait dissection and predictive molecular breeding technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rusk &amp; Nicole Cheap third-generation sequencing. Nat. Methods 6, 244â244 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d323e678">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Choi &amp; Chul, S. On the study of microbial transcriptomes using second- and third-generation sequencing technologies. J. Microbiol. 54, 527â536 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d323e681">16</a></sup>. Despite the high economic value of and increasing industrial demand for alfalfa, improvements through breeding are very limited, partly due to a lack of information on the whole genome. Alfalfa is suggested to be an autotetraploid (2<i>n</i>â=â4<i>x</i>â=â32) subspecies in the <i>M. sativa</i> complex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Matheson, N. K., Small, D. M. &amp; Copeland, L. Î²- d-mannanases in germinating lucerne (alfalfa) seeds. Carbohyd. Res. 82, 325â331 (1980)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d323e694">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yu, C. Y., Dong, J. G., Hu, S. W. &amp; Xu, A. X. Exposure to trace amounts of sulfonylurea herbicide tribenuron-methyl causes male sterility in 17 species or subspecies of cruciferous plants. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d323e697">18</a></sup>. The recently published genome assembly of an autotetraploid alfalfa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, H. et al. Allele-aware chromosome-level genome assembly and efficient transgene-free genome editing for the autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa. Nat. Commun. 11, 2494 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d323e702">19</a></sup> is expected to greatly facilitate the future improvement of molecular breeding strategies. However, assembling a complete autotetraploid genome is still challenging due to essential features of tetrasomic inheritance, as more than 400âMb of contigs were not placed onto the chromosomes in the above genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, H. et al. Allele-aware chromosome-level genome assembly and efficient transgene-free genome editing for the autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa. Nat. Commun. 11, 2494 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d323e706">19</a></sup>. In this case, assembling the genome of the diploid progenitor could be an alternative way to obtain full genomic information for alfalfa. Indeed, genomic information for diploid progenitors has provided substantial insights into selection for several key agronomic traits and the evolutionary history of multiple polyploid food crops, such as cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Huang, G. et al. Genome sequence of Gossypium herbaceum and genome updates of Gossypium arboreum and Gossypium hirsutum provide insights into cotton A-genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 52, 516â524 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d323e710">20</a></sup>, wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d323e714">21</a></sup>, and strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Author correction: origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 765 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d323e718">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Previous studies have demonstrated that <i>M. sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â16), a perennial self-incompatible herb, is the diploid progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Small, E. &amp; Jomphe, M. A synopsis of the genus Medicago (Leguminosae). Can. J. Bot. 67, 3260â3294 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d323e737">23</a></sup>. In this study, we assembled a chromosome-scale draft genome of <i>M. sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i> voucher PI464715 (hereafter PI464715) using a combination of Illumina, Hi-C and Nanopore sequencing technologies. Using this high-quality genome, we further performed genome annotation, evolutionary analysis, and comparative genomics and identified resistance genes and genes involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways. Our PI464715 genome assembly provides a diploid reference for analyzing the alfalfa genome and is a valuable resource for future molecular breeding of alfalfa. This genome is also beneficial for investigating genome evolution in the genus <i>Medicago</i> and related taxa.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequence and assembly</h3><p><i>Medicago sativa</i> spp<i>. caerulea</i> (voucher PI464715; 2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â16) was chosen for genome sequencing and assembly. A genome survey was first performed to assess the genome size based on 81.5âGb of Illumina data. Using K-mer analysis, we evaluated the genome size to be ~802âMb, with a high level of heterozygosity of 1.9% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">1</a>). To accurately assemble this highly heterozygous genome, Illumina, Nanopore and Hi-C technologies were adopted for sequencing, and a series of methods were performed for assembly. Based on 116.5âGb of Nanopore long reads corresponding to ~145Ã coverage of the estimated ~802âMb genome, we preliminarily obtained a raw assembled genome of 1,345.8âMb and contig N50 of 2.8âMb by the NextGraph module. After polishing by NextPolish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hu, J., Fan, J., Sun, Z. &amp; Liu, S. NextPolish: a fast and efficient genome polishing tool for long-read assembly. Bioinformatics 36, 2253â2255 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d323e781">24</a></sup> and performing deredundancy by purge_haplotigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Roach, M. J., Schmidt, S. A. &amp; Borneman, A. R. Purge Haplotigs: allelic contig reassignment for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d323e785">25</a></sup>, we obtained the final genome assembly with a length of 793.2âMb and a contig number of 355 and contig N50 of 3.86âMb, constituting 98.9% of the predicted genome size (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S1</a>). We used the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) evaluation score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d323e795">26</a></sup> to assess the quality of the assembly, which resulted in 97.7% gene set completeness (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S2</a>), indicating a very complete and high-quality genome assembly. We further connected 338 (95.2%) out of 355 contigs into eight pseudochromosomes based on ~224âGb of Hi-C data (~279Ãcoverage) using the hierarchical clustering strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d323e803">27</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S3</a>). In total, 98.5% (781âMb) of the assembly was anchored and oriented on eight pseudochromosomes, which ranged from 83.24 to 118.42âMb in length (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S3</a>), and 98.3% of transcriptomic reads and 96.4% of Illumina short reads could be properly mapped to the final genome assembly (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the PI464715 genome assembly and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Our PI464715 assembly provided significant improvement (with larger contig sizes and a higher BUSCO score) than the alfalfa genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, H. et al. Allele-aware chromosome-level genome assembly and efficient transgene-free genome editing for the autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa. Nat. Commun. 11, 2494 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d323e1465">19</a></sup>. Our genome has a contig N50 of 3.86âMb, which is ~8.4-fold greater than that of the alfalfa genome (459âkb). Moreover, we placed 781âMb of the assembly onto eight chromosomes with the aid of Hi-C data, while in the alfalfa genome, only 685âMb (on average across the four assembled monoploid genomes) was anchored on the eight chromosomes. Our assembled genome also obtained a higher BUSCO evaluation score (97.7%) than the four monoploid genomes of alfalfa (88.5%, 88.3%, 87.5%, and 87.2%). All these comparisons indicated that our genome has better contiguity and higher quality.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>In total, we identified 47,202 protein-coding genes, with an average gene length of 3151âbp (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">3</a>), based on a combined strategy using de novo, transcriptome-based and homology-based methods. The total GC content of the PI464715 genome assembly was 34.21% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S3</a>). BUSCO evaluation further showed that the annotated PI464715 genome contained 97% BUSCOs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S6</a>). Then, five protein databases, namely, InterPro, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG), SwissProt, KOG and NR, were used to compare our protein models. Overall, we assigned potential functions to 92.51% (43,669) of the protein-coding genes in the PI464715 genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S7</a>). The gene distribution and GC content along each chromosome were calculated, and their distributions were uneven (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig2">2b, d</a>), as also found in many other plant species (e.g.<i>, M. truncatula</i>). In addition, we identified 857 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 1023 microRNAs (miRNAs), 1978 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), and 2438 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) in the PI464715 genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig2">2eâh</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Overview of the PI464715 genome assembly."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Overview of the PI464715 genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="683" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Genomic positions (in Mb) of the eight chromosomes of PI464715. <b>b</b> Gene density. <b>c</b> Transposable element (TE) content. <b>d</b> Guanine-cytosine (GC) content. <b>e</b> miRNA density. <b>f</b> tRNA density. <b>g</b> snRNA density. <b>h</b> rRNA density. <b>i</b> Interconnections of paralogs in the genome are represented with colored lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We annotated repetitive sequences of the genome using both de novo and homology-based approaches. We annotated ~440âMb (55.55%) of the PI464715 genome assembly that comprised transposable elements (TEs), of which long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were the most abundant, accounting for 57.2% of TEs and 31.75% of the assembled genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig2">2c</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a>). DNA transposons, long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs) and short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs) accounted for 7.24%, 4.34%, and 0.32% of the genome assembly, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene-family analysis</h3><p>To investigate the genome evolution of PI464715, annotated genes from 11 species of the Leguminosae family (i.e., M. truncatula, Trifolium pertense, Pisum sativum, Cicer arietinum, Lotus japonicus, Phaseolus vulgaris, Glycine max, Cajanus cajan, Lupinus angustifolius, Arachis duranensis, and Arachis ipaensis) and one rosid species (Arabidopsis thaliana) were clustered into gene families. In total, 38,375 PI464715 genes (81.3%) were clustered into 18,434 gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig3">3c</a>). PI464715 shared a total of 12,157 (65.9%) gene families with the 12 other species and contained 579 (3.1%) unique gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig3">3a,c</a>). We determined and selected 553 single-copy orthologous genes from these 13 species for subsequent phylogenetic analysis. As expected, PI464715 displayed a close relationship with M. truncatula and phylogenetically diverged from its common ancestor ~5.12 million years ago (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig3">3c</a>). The phylogenetic relationships among these 13 species were the same as those recovered from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kreplak, J. et al. A reference genome for pea provides insight into legume genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 51, 1411â1422 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d323e1581">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic and evolutionary analyses of the PI464715 genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic and evolutionary analyses of the PI464715 genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="613" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Venn diagram of shared orthologous gene families in PI464715, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>P. sativum</i>, and <i>G. max</i>. The number of gene families is listed for each component. <b>b</b> Distribution of synonymous substitution levels (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of syntenic orthologous (dashed curves) and paralogous genes (solid curves) after evolutionary rate correction. Mtr: <i>M. truncatula</i>; Car: <i>C. arietinum</i>; Gmax: <i>G. max</i>; Lan: <i>L. angustifolius</i>; Msa: PI464715. MsaA: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 1. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of 13 plant species and the evolution of gene families. The blue numerical value beside each node shows the estimated divergence time of each node (MYA, million years ago). The numbers of gene-family expansions, contractions and significant (<i>p</i>-valueââ¤â0.01) gene-family expansion and contraction events are indicated by red, black and green numbers, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among the 18,434 gene families identified in PI464715, 3468 expanded and 2464 contracted gene families were detected. Compared with its close relative <i>M. truncatula</i> (another important species in the genus used as a legume model species), which exhibited 1858 expanded and 1576 contracted gene families, PI464715 had more gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig3">3c</a>). Furthermore, a higher number of gene families in PI464715 compared with <i>M. truncatula</i> (i.e., 479 vs. 336) exhibited significant rapid evolution (family-wide <i>p</i>-valueââ¤â0.01) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig2">2c</a>). The GO enrichment analysis suggested that reproductive processes, such as recognition of pollen (GO:0048544), pollen-pistil interaction (GO:0009875) and pollination (GO:0009856), were enriched in both the contracted and expanded gene families (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S9</a>), and these genes may be involved in the transition between self-compatibility in <i>M. truncatula</i> and self-incompatibility in PI464715. The GO enrichment analysis of the expanded gene families also suggested multiple response pathways (e.g., response to chemical, response to hormone, response to auxin and response to stimulus), all of which may be related to the adaptation of this species to diverse environments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative genomic analyses and genome expansion in PI464715</h3><p>Synteny analysis was conducted between the PI464715 genome assembly, the four monoploid genomes of alfalfa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, H. et al. Allele-aware chromosome-level genome assembly and efficient transgene-free genome editing for the autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa. Nat. Commun. 11, 2494 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d323e1685">19</a></sup> and the <i>M. truncatula</i> ecotype Jemalong A17 genome v5.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Pecrix, Y. et al. Whole-genome landscape of Medicago truncatula symbiotic genes. Nat. Plants 4, 1017â1025 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d323e1692">29</a></sup> to explore their evolution. High collinearity was revealed between our genome with all four subgenomes of alfalfa and for five chromosomes between our genome and the A17 genome by visualizing syntenic blocks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig4">4</a>). We further detected a pair of large interchromosomal rearrangements between chromosome 4 and chromosome 8 and a large inversion on chromosome 1, as also evident in the dot plots comparing our genome and the A17 genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig4">4a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">4</a>). Such rearrangements and inversions were also found between the genomes of two ecotypes, A17 and R108<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Karen et al. Strategies for optimizing BioNano and Dovetail explored through a second reference quality assembly for the legume model, Medicago truncatula. BMC Genomics 18, 578 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d323e1706">30</a></sup>, but not between the PI464715 and R108 genomes (result not shown). These results indicate that the large interchromosomal rearrangements and inversion may have occurred specifically in A17 after the divergence between <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>M. sativa</i>, but this needs further investigation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gene synteny between the M. truncatula ecotype Jemalong A17, PI464715, and alfalfa genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Gene synteny between the <i>M. truncatula</i> ecotype Jemalong A17, PI464715, and alfalfa genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="427" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Synteny comparison of homologous chromosomes between the PI464715 genome and the <i>M. truncatula</i> A17 genome. <b>b</b> Synteny comparison of homologous chromosomes between the PI464715 genome and the <i>M. sativa</i> genome (MsaA: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 1; MsaD: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 4). <b>c</b> Synteny comparison of homologous chromosomes between the PI464715 genome and the <i>M. sativa</i> genome (MsaB: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 2, MsaC: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 3).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Our assembled PI464715 genome is 793âMb in size, almost two times larger than the genome of <i>M. truncatula</i> (420âMb). We tested whether whole-genome duplication (WGD) events accounted for the genome expansion in PI464715. We selected the genomes of four species (i.e<i>., M. truncatula</i>, <i>C. arietinum</i>, <i>G. max</i>, and <i>L. angustifolius</i>) from the Leguminosae family and subgenome A of alfalfa and performed comparative genomic analysis with PI464715 to investigate the WGD events and divergence time between PI464715 and other species, which were evaluated by measuring the synonymous nucleotide substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of orthologous gene pairs. All six species displayed a peak <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value of 0.62, consistent with the finding of a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, J. et al. Hierarchically aligning 10 legume genomes establishes a family-level genomics platform. Plant Physiol. 174, 284â300 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d323e1797">31</a></sup>, and the divergence between PI464715 and the other four species occurred afterwards, suggesting a common whole-genome duplication event for all Papilionoideae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Young, N. D., DebellÃ©, F., Oldroyd, G. E. D., Geurts, R. &amp; Roe, B. A. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d323e1801">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Cannon, S. B. et al. Multiple polyploidy events in the early radiation of nodulating and nonnodulating legumes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 193â210 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d323e1804">33</a></sup>. PI464715 and <i>M. truncatula</i> experienced no WGD events after their divergence, and the divergence between PI464715 and the diploid ancestor of alfalfa (i.e., represented by one monoploid genome, subgenome A) was the most recent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Resistance-related (R) genes</h3><p>Plant resistance genes (<i>R</i> genes) are important gene groups that usually include an NBS (nucleotide-binding site) domain and an LRR (leucine-rich repeat) domain and play a crucial role in plant disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lozano, R., Hamblin, M. T., Prochnik, S. &amp; Jannink, J. L. Identification and distribution of the NBS-LRR gene family in the Cassava genome. BMC Genomics 16, 360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d323e1827">34</a></sup>. Based on the types of domains in the N-terminal region, <i>R</i> genes belong to three groups: CNL (CC-NBS-LRR), RNL (RPW8-NBS-LRR) and TNL (TIR-NBS-LRR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Xiang, L. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of NBS-encoding genes in four Gossypium species. BMC Genomics 18, 292 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d323e1834">35</a></sup>. In the PI464615 genome, 489 <i>R</i> genes were detected, including 117 CNL genes, 58 TNL genes and 11 RNL genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S10</a>). The numbers of <i>R</i> genes detected in the four monoploid genomes of alfalfa were similar but slightly smaller than those in the PI464615 genome. In total, 1749â<i>R</i> genes were detected in the autotetraploid alfalfa genome. Furthermore, PI464615 had ~1.5-fold to ~2.2-fold more TNL genes but fewer CNL genes than the four monoploid genomes of alfalfa. More <i>R</i> genes (692) were detected in <i>M. truncatula</i>, including 139 CNL genes, 145 TNL genes and 15 RNL genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S10</a>). <i>R</i> genes with complete domains identified from the PI464715 and <i>M. truncatula</i> genomes were selected to construct a phylogenetic tree. The results indicated that these <i>R</i> genes were clustered into the RNL, TNL and CNL groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="hylogenetic tree of the nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domain R gene-family identified in the PI464715 genome and M. sativa A subgenome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: hylogenetic tree of the nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domain R gene-family identified in the PI464715 genome and <i>M. sativa</i> A subgenome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="598" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(MsaA: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 1). Class represents the three groups of <i>R</i> genes. MsaA: <i>M. sativa</i> subgenome 1.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Lignin and cellulose biosynthesis-related genes</h3><p>The content of lignin and cellulose is one of the important factors affecting alfalfa quality as an animal feed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Reddy, M. S. et al. Targeted downâregulation of cytochrome P450 enzymes for forage quality improvement in alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 16573â16578 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d323e1917">36</a></sup>, and reducing the lignin content in alfalfa can improve digestibility and, correspondingly, animal performance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Barros, J., Temple, S. &amp; Dixon, R. A. Development and commercialization of reduced lignin alfalfa. Curr. Opin. Biotech. 56, 48â54 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d323e1921">37</a></sup>. Based on a BLASTp homology search and Pfam analysis, we identified a total of 82 putative lignin biosynthesis-related genes and 85 putative cellulose biosynthesis-related genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig6">6</a>). These genes were unevenly distributed on the eight chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">6</a>). Hierarchical cluster analysis of transcriptomic data showed clustering of the three repeats for the leaf or stem (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">7</a>). Transcriptomic analysis revealed that the expression patterns of these identified genes involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways in leaf and stem tissues were similar, but the expression levels were slightly higher in stem than in leaf tissue (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig6">6</a>), which is consistent with the fact that the lignin content is higher in stem than in leaf tissue. We also found that the expression levels of multiple gene copies for each gene were different. For example, among the seven gene copies that putatively encode the enzyme HCT, <i>MsaG017994</i> had the highest expression level, which was 13â250 times higher than that of other gene copies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Fig6">6a</a>). Knowing the relative expression levels of different gene copies can be useful when conducting targeted downregulation of enzymes for forage quality improvement by reducing lignin content, for example<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Reddy, M. S. et al. Targeted downâregulation of cytochrome P450 enzymes for forage quality improvement in alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 16573â16578 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d323e1944">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Shadle, G. et al. Down-regulation of hydroxycinnamoyl CoA: Shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase in transgenic alfalfa affects lignification, development and forage quality. Phytochemistry 68, 1521â1529 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d323e1947">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bhattarai, K. et al. Agronomic performance and lignin content of HCT down-regulated alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.). Bioenerg. Res. 11, 505â515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d323e1950">39</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Expression of lignin and cellulose biosynthesis genes in leaf and stem tissues."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Expression of lignin and cellulose biosynthesis genes in leaf and stem tissues.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00417-7/MediaObjects/41438_2020_417_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="318" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Simplified diagram of lignin biosynthesis pathways in plants modified from Vanholme et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Vanholme, R., De Meester, B., Ralph, J. &amp; Boerjan, W. Lignin biosynthesis and its integration into metabolism. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 56, 230â239 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d323e1968">78</a></sup> and a heat map showing the expression of lignin biosynthesis-related genes in leaf and stem tissues. Values within brackets indicate the numbers of gene copies corresponding to the catalytic genes in the pathways. The enzymes involved in the lignin biosynthesis pathway are phenylalanine ammonia lyase (PAL), cinnamate 4-hydroxylase (C4H), 4-coumarate-CoA ligase (4CL), cinnamoyl-CoA reductase (CCR), cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase (CAD), hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase (HCT), p-coumaroyl shikimate/quinate 3â-hydroxylase (C3âH), caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase (CCoAOMT), ferulate 5-hydroxylase (F5H), caffeic acid O-methyltransferase (COMT), peroxidase (PER), laccase (LAC) and caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE). <b>b</b> Heat map showing the expression of cellulose-related genes in leaf and stem tissues. The enzymes involved in cellulose biosynthesis pathways are cellulose synthase (CESA), korrigan (KOR), chitinase-like (CTL), cobra (COB), cobra-like (COBL), kobito (KOB), stello (STL), cellulose synthase-microtubule uncoupling protein (CMU), companion of cellulose synthase (CC), theseus (THE), cellulose synthase interacting (POM), and tracheary element differentiation-related (TED).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p><i>Medicago</i> includes economically important forage crops, such as alfalfa (<i>M. sativa</i>) and âJinhuacaiâ (<i>M. polymorpha</i>), in addition to a model organism (<i>M. truncatula</i>) in plant biology. Despite the importance of the genus, genomic resources are relatively scarce, and genome sequences are available only for <i>M. truncatula</i> and alfalfa, which largely slows down progress towards understanding the genome evolution and genetic code underlying molecular breeding for major crops in this genus. Here, we describe a chromosome-scale assembly of the <i>M. sativa</i> ssp. <i>caerulea</i> genome (i.e., the diploid progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa) obtained by a combination of data from the Illumina, Nanopore and Hi-C platforms. The genome assembly was 793âMb in length, and &gt;98.5% of the assembled genome was placed on eight chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#MOESM1">S5</a>). The BUSCO assessment revealed 97.7% complete genes in the assembled genome, which represents a more contiguous and higher-quality genome assembly than that recently published for alfalfa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, H. et al. Allele-aware chromosome-level genome assembly and efficient transgene-free genome editing for the autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa. Nat. Commun. 11, 2494 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d323e2019">19</a></sup>. Our results further reveal that Nanopore long reads with the aid of Hi-C data can be adopted to accurately assemble a highly heterozygous and repetitive genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kang, M. et al. A chromosome-scale genome assembly of Isatis indigotica, an important medicinal plant used in traditional Chinese medicine. Hortic. Res. 7, 18 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d323e2023">40</a></sup>.</p><p>The PI464615 genome (793âMb) is approximately twofold larger than that of the closely related species <i>M. truncatula</i> (420âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Pecrix, Y. et al. Whole-genome landscape of Medicago truncatula symbiotic genes. Nat. Plants 4, 1017â1025 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d323e2033">29</a></sup>. Several factors, including transposable elements (TEs) and whole-genome duplication (WGD), have been proposed to account for variation in genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tiley, G. P. &amp; Burleigh, J. G. The relationship of recombination rate, genome structure, and patterns of molecular evolution across angiosperms. BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 194 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d323e2037">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Vitte, C. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analysis of retrotransposon structural diversity uncovers properties and propensities in angiosperm genome evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 17638â17643 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d323e2040">42</a></sup>. Recent analyses have shown that WGD or polyploidization seems to have occurred during the evolutionary histories of most plant species, such as the Î³ event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wu, S., Han, B. &amp; Jiao, Y. Genetic contribution of paleopolyploidy to adaptive evolution in angiosperms. Mol. Plant 13, 59â71 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d323e2044">43</a></sup> that occurred ~140â150âMyr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d323e2048">44</a></sup> and is shared by all eudicots. After the Î³-event, some species experienced no WGD events, such as grape and coffee, whereas other species, such as <i>M. truncatula</i>, kiwifruit and <i>Asparagus setaceus</i>, may have undergone one or two additional rounds of WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Pecrix, Y. et al. Whole-genome landscape of Medicago truncatula symbiotic genes. Nat. Plants 4, 1017â1025 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d323e2059">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, S. F. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly, annotation and evolutionary analysis of the ornamental plant Asparagus setaceus. Hortic. Res. 7, 48 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d323e2062">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wu, H. et al. A high-quality Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) genome. Hortic. Res. 6, 117 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d323e2065">46</a></sup>. All Papilionoideae within the Leguminosae family share a common WGD event, after which most species experienced no WGD events, except for <i>G. max</i> and <i>L. angustifolius</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barker, D. G. et al. Medicago truncatula, a model plant for studying the molecular genetics of the Rhizobium-legume symbiosis. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 8, 40â49 (1990)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d323e2074">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cook, D. R. Medicago truncatula-A model in the making! Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 2, 301â304 (1999)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d323e2074_1">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kreplak, J. et al. A reference genome for pea provides insight into legume genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 51, 1411â1422 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d323e2077">49</a></sup>. Our results from <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution analysis reveal that both the PI464615 and <i>M. truncatula</i> genomes have only one peak, which precedes the divergence of the two species and is consistent with the ancestral Papilionoideae WGD event. The proliferation of TEs is another factor accounting for genome expansion. In this study, we identified ~440âMb TEs, constituting 55.5% of the assembled PI464615 genome, which is ~234âMb larger than the total length of TEs (~206âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Pecrix, Y. et al. Whole-genome landscape of Medicago truncatula symbiotic genes. Nat. Plants 4, 1017â1025 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d323e2089">29</a></sup> in the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome. Therefore, the proliferation of TEs rather than WGD and the presence of more expanded gene families than in <i>M. truncatula</i> resulted in genome expansion in PI464615.</p><p>In summary, we report a high-quality chromosome-level reference genome for <i>M. sativa</i> ssp. <i>caerulea</i> (voucher PI464715). We assembled a 793âMb genome and annotated 47,202 protein-coding genes. We also identified resistance genes in the PI464715 genome and in each of the four monoploid genomes of alfalfa, which may provide a genetic basis for understanding the gain of resistance-related traits in alfalfa. We further identified 82 and 85 candidate genes that may be involved in the lignin and cellulose biosynthesis pathways, respectively, and described the expression profiles of these genes in leaf and stem tissues. Such information will be very useful for improving alfalfa quality in the future, for example, by the downregulation of targeted enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Reddy, M. S. et al. Targeted downâregulation of cytochrome P450 enzymes for forage quality improvement in alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 16573â16578 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d323e2108">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Shadle, G. et al. Down-regulation of hydroxycinnamoyl CoA: Shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase in transgenic alfalfa affects lignification, development and forage quality. Phytochemistry 68, 1521â1529 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d323e2111">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bhattarai, K. et al. Agronomic performance and lignin content of HCT down-regulated alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.). Bioenerg. Res. 11, 505â515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d323e2114">39</a></sup> or through gene editing to decrease lignin content. The available genome sequence for the direct progenitor of autotetraploid alfalfa is an important complement to the alfalfa genome and holds great promise for further understanding fundamental aspects of genomic architecture and improving molecular breeding strategies in alfalfa. The genomic resource is also highly valuable for evolutionary studies in related species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Material and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Material and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials, DNA extraction, and estimation of genome size</h3><p>Seeds of <i>M. sativa</i> spp<i>. caerule</i>a voucher PI464715 were obtained from the National Plant Germplasm System (NPGS) of the United States Department of Agriculture (USDA) and planted in a greenhouse. Fresh leaves of a growing plant cultivated in a greenhouse were used to extract genomic DNA using a DNA Secure Plant Kit (Tiangen Biotech, Co., Ltd., Beijing, China). Paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 270âbp were constructed, and the Illumina HiSeqâXâTen platform was used to generate Illumina short reads, which were first used to estimate genome size. We generated ~81.5âGb of reads and determined the abundance of 17-K-mers in the generated Illumina data using Kmerfreq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d323e2136">50</a></sup>. K-mer curve fitting was also performed under different gradient combinations of heterozygosity to estimate the heterozygosity of the genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Total genomic DNA was fractionated into 10â50âkb fragments with BluePippin, which was used to construct the libraries following the Nanopore library construction protocol. The generated libraries were then submitted for sequencing at the Nextomics Biosciences Company (Wuhan, China) using the GridION X5 sequencer platform (Oxford Nanopore Technologies, UK). The quality-controlled reads were used for assembly with the software Nextdenovo v. 2.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Lin, H. H., Liao, Y. C. &amp; Dutilh, B. E. Evaluation and validation of assembling corrected Pacbio long reads for microbial genome completion via hybrid approaches. PLoS ONE 10, e0144305 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d323e2148">51</a></sup> following three steps. First, the NextCorrect module was applied to correct sequencing errors. Second, a preliminary assembly was generated based on the NextGraph module, which resulted in a genome size of 1345.8âMb, with a contig number of 1154 and contig N50 of 2.8âMb. Then, we polished the preliminary assembly using the Nextpolish v. 1.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hu, J., Fan, J., Sun, Z. &amp; Liu, S. NextPolish: a fast and efficient genome polishing tool for long-read assembly. Bioinformatics 36, 2253â2255 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d323e2152">24</a></sup> module. At this stage, Nanopore long reads and Illumina short reads were used repetitively three times for genome correction. Finally, allelic haplotigs were removed using purge_haplotigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Roach, M. J., Schmidt, S. A. &amp; Borneman, A. R. Purge Haplotigs: allelic contig reassignment for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d323e2156">25</a></sup> software to obtain the final genome sequence. BUSCO v. 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d323e2160">26</a></sup>, with 1,350 genes from Embryophyta odb10, was used to evaluate the completeness and accuracy of the assembled genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Chromosome-scale assembly with Hi-C data</h3><p>Approximately 2âg of fresh leaves collected from the same PI464715 accession was used for Hi-C sequencing. Hi-C libraries were constructed following Miele et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Miele, A. &amp; Dekker, J. Mapping cis- and trans- chromatin interaction networks using chromosome conformation capture (3C). Methods Mol. Biol. 464, 105â121 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d323e2172">52</a></sup> with chromatin extraction; digestion; and DNA ligation, purification and fragmentation. Hi-C sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform (Illumina, CA, USA). A preliminary assembly was carried out to correct errors in contigs by splitting contigs into 100âkb segments on average. BWA v. 0.7.17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589â595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00417-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d323e2176">53</a></sup> was used to map the Hi-C data to these segments. The uniquely mapped Hi-C data were retained, clustered, ordered and placed onto the eight pseudochromosomes using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kreplak, J. et al. A reference genome for pea provides insight into legume genome evolution. Nat.
